                gptText = gptText.replace(/\&/g, '%26');
                return gptText.replace(/\^|\*|#|\$|\"|\'|\=|\,|\!|\+|\~|\;|\(|\)|\<|\>|\[|\]|\?|\%|\:|\@|\;|\&|\||\{|\}|\\|/g, '');
            }
            return "blank";
        }

        googletag.pubads()
            .setTargeting('property', 'value-of-property')    // Change and duplicate this line for as many additional keywords to send
            .setTargeting('doi', gptClean('10.1002/adma.202307645')) // Publication DOI
            .setTargeting('pbPage', gptClean(gptPage))
            .setTargeting('pbSubPage', gptClean(gptSubPage))
            .setTargeting('urlpath', gptClean(document.location.pathname));

        googletag.defineSlot($GooglePublisherCategory, 'fluid', 'advert-leaderboard')
            .defineSizeMapping(sizes.leaderboard)
            .addService(googletag.pubads())
            .setCollapseEmptyDiv(true);

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="0fca3522e88441fdbbf018682537b990">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: 'd03b0cd6-c836-494f-9e78-a6ef8d390572'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d124509849" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Sodium metal has become one of the most promising anodes for next-generation cheap and high-energy-density metal batteries; however, challenges caused by the uncontrollable sodium dendrite growth and fragile solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) restrict their large-scale practical applications in low-cost and wide-voltage-window carbonate electrolytes. Herein, a novel multifunctional separator with lightweight and high thinness is proposed, assembled by the cobalt-based metal–organic framework nanowires (Co-NWS), to replace the widely applied thick and heavy glass fiber separator. Benefitting from its abundant sodiophilic functional groups and densely stacked nanowires, Co-NWS not only exhibits outstanding electrolyte wettability and effectively induces uniform Na<sup>+</sup> ion flux as a strong ion redistributor but also favors constructing the robust N,F-rich SEI layer. Satisfactorily, with 10&nbsp;µL carbonate electrolyte, a Na|Co-NWS|Cu half-cell delivers stable cycling (over 260 cycles) with a high average Coulombic efficiency of 98%, and the symmetric cell shows a long cycle life of more than 500&nbsp;h. Remarkably, the full cell shows a long-term life span (over 1500 cycles with 92% capacity retention) at high current density in the carbonate electrolyte. This work opens up a strategy for developing dendrite-free, low-cost, and long-life-span sodium metal batteries in carbonate-based electrolytes.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adma202307645-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adma202307645-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Daily worsening environmental issues and the rapid development of smart grid-scale energy storage, electric vehicles, and portable electronics remarkably urge the demand for high-energy-density, low-cost, and safe energy storage systems.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202307645-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202307645-bib-0001_R_d124509684e470" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Adv. Mater.">1</a>-<a href="#adma202307645-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202307645-bib-0004_R_d124509684e470" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Nano Res.">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Although sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have been considered a promising alternative for lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) due to the abundant reservation and low price of sodium, their energy density is still lower than that of LIBs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202307645-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202307645-bib-0005_R_d124509684e475" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">5</a>-<a href="#adma202307645-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202307645-bib-0007_R_d124509684e475" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Adv. Energy Mater.">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Recently, attention has been drawn toward the application of sodium metal anodes (SMAs) in SIBs to develop sodium metal batteries (SMBs), thanks to their high theoretical specific capacity (1165 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>) and low redox potential (−2.714&nbsp;V vs standard hydrogen electrode).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202307645-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202307645-bib-0008_R_d124509684e483" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Nat. Mater.">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Unfortunately, SMBs still face challenges, such as uncontrollable sodium (Na) dendrite growth, massive volume expansion, and fragile solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) layers, considerably limiting their commercialization.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202307645-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202307645-bib-0009_R_d124509684e488" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Nat. Nanotechnol.">9</a>, <a href="#adma202307645-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202307645-bib-0010_R_d124509684e488" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Adv. Mater.">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> To address those dilemmas, various strategies have been proposed, including constructing advanced host materials, designing artificial protection layers, introducing electrolyte additives, and modifying/fabricating separators.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202307645-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202307645-bib-0011_R_d124509684e493" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - New Carbon Mater.">11</a>-<a href="#adma202307645-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202307645-bib-0014_R_d124509684e493" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Adv. Mater.">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Although those approaches could further restrict the growth of Na dendrites and thus enhance the Coulombic efficiency (CE) and life span of SMBs, most of them cannot full-chain blocking the Na dendrites, i.e., reducing the nucleation possibility of Na dendrites, limiting the growth of Na dendrites, and protecting from the piercing by the Na dendrites at the same time. Besides, they also hardly struggle to strike a balance between extended life span and improved specific energy density.</p>
         
         <p>Currently, ether electrolytes, with their relatively higher lowest unoccupied molecular orbital, induce fewer side effects than carbonate ester electrolytes, enabling SMBs to achieve more stable Na plating/stripping cycles with higher CE values and longer life span, whereas their shortages, such as high price and flammability, limited voltage window, greatly hinder their further wide application.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202307645-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202307645-bib-0015_R_d124509684e501" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Adv. Sci.">15</a>-<a href="#adma202307645-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202307645-bib-0017_R_d124509684e501" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Energy Storage Mater.">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Carbonate ester electrolytes, on the other hand, comprising the wider electrochemical window, low cost, and high boiling/flashing point, have been considered as the promising electrolyte system for commercialized SMBs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202307645-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202307645-bib-0018_R_d124509684e506" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Energy Environ. Sci.">18</a>, <a href="#adma202307645-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202307645-bib-0019_R_d124509684e506" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Ind. Eng. Chem. Res.">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Unfortunately, establishing stable SEI layers in carbonate-based electrolytes is challenging due to the high reactivity between the SMAs and the electrolytes, resulting in relatively low reversibility, short life span, and uncontrollable Na dendrite growth on the SMAs, making it far from enough to meet the practical requirements of SMBs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202307645-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202307645-bib-0020_R_d124509684e511" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Thus, seeking a multifunctional way to construct robust SEI layers and restrict dendrite growth simultaneously in the carbonate-based electrolyte is urgent for developing long-term operation SMBs.</p>
         
         <p>As an essential part of the battery, separators act as a bridge to contact between anodes and cathodes via ion transportation, providing an increased likelihood for simultaneously regulating the Na<sup>+</sup> ion transmission, SEI formation, and Na plating/stripping behaviors.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202307645-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202307645-bib-0021_R_d124509684e522" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Currently, polyolefin compounds such as polypropylene (PP), polyethylene (PE), related PE/PP composite materials, and glass fibers are widely used as separators in SMBs due to their good chemical stability and low-cost mass production. As nonpolar polyolefin separators are inherently hydrophobic, they possess poor wettability and electrolyte retention to widely used polar carbonate electrolytes, such as propylene carbonate (PC), ethylene carbonate (EC), γ-butyrolactone, etc.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202307645-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202307645-bib-0022_R_d124509684e527" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - J. Electroanal. Chem.">22</a>, <a href="#adma202307645-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202307645-bib-0023_R_d124509684e527" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Consequently, the glass fiber (GF) separator becomes the only viable option when applying these polar carbonate electrolytes. Nevertheless, the GF separator suffers from a large thickness (over hundreds of micrometers), excessive porosity, and fluffy structure, necessitating a considerable amount of liquid electrolyte to fill all the spaces. This results in decreased gravimetric/volumetric energy densities, increased safety risks from flammable solvents, and higher costs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202307645-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202307645-bib-0021_R_d124509684e533" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">21</a>, <a href="#adma202307645-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202307645-bib-0024_R_d124509684e533" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Even worse, the fluffy structure with its large pores offers ample space for dendrites to pierce through, decreasing CE and increasing the risk of short circuits and thermal runaway.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202307645-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202307645-bib-0001_R_d124509684e538" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Adv. Mater.">1</a>, <a href="#adma202307645-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202307645-bib-0021_R_d124509684e538" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Until now, although some modified GF or polyolefin separators via surface-engineering strategies have enabled the metal batteries to achieve better life span, the required amount of carbonate electrolyte is still significant (≥40&nbsp;µL), and the CE values are still relatively low. Thus, improving the CE value and life span of SMBs while reducing the amount of carbonate electrolytes to enhance safety is a significant challenge for SMBs' commercialization.</p>
         
         <p>Herein, a multifunctionalized separator, based on cobalt-based metal–organic framework nanowires (Co-NWS), has been proposed and successfully enables the corresponding SMBs to achieve high average CE and long-term cycling performance with a reduced carbonate electrolyte volume via full-chain blocking the Na dendrites. Taking advantage of the abundant functional sodiophilic groups and the densely packed spatial structure, the Co-NWS separators not only exhibit outstanding wettability to carbonate electrolytes and serve as a robust ion redistributor to regulate uniform Na<sup>+</sup> ion transportation, thus significantly improving the utilization of electrolyte and dramatically reducing the nucleation of Na dendrites, but also could effectively induce the formation of N,F-rich SEI layers, realizing higher Na<sup>+</sup> ion conductivity and satisfied mechanical strength to limit the growth of Na dendrites. Plus, its high Young's modulus could forcefully protect itself from piercing by the Na dendrites. Based on those advantages, Co-NWS could result in up to 98% average CE value and over 500&nbsp;h stable cycling for the half-cell and the symmetric cell with 10&nbsp;µL carbonate electrolyte, respectively. To our best knowledge, this is the highest CE value achieved by the half-cell optimized through separator engineering after cycling over 200 cycles in the ester-based electrolyte (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information). When coupling with Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (NVP) cathodes, the full cell with Co-NWS could deliver over 1500 stable cycles with a 99.8% average CE value and 92% capacity retention under a high rate of 5 C. Even at a limited negative/positive areal capacity ratio (N/P ratio), the full cell could conduct stable cycling over 170 times with high CE and retention values. Furthermore, Co-NWS's lightweight, high-thinness, and less-electrolyte-usage properties could vastly increase the gravimetric/volumetric energy densities of full cells. This work offers an efficient strategy for realizing high-safety, high-reversibility, high-energy-density, and long-life-span SMBs with carbonate electrolytes.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adma202307645-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adma202307645-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <p>The self-support Co-NWS could be easily assembled via filtering Co-based nanowires (Co–nitrilotriacetic acid (NTA)) synthesized by the one-step solvent-thermal reaction (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma202307645-fig-0001">1a</a>), followed by vacuum drying. It is worth noting that the synthesis could not involve any binders and the corresponding volatile organic compounds, e.g., polyvinylidene fluoride (PVDF) and <i>N</i>-methyl-pyrrolidone, decreasing the production cost, avoiding the use of more environmentally unfriendly solvent, favoring the recycling process for the components at the end of battery life.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202307645-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202307645-bib-0025_R_d124509684e579" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - J Solid State Electrochem.">25</a>-<a href="#adma202307645-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202307645-bib-0027_R_d124509684e579" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Adv. Energy Mater.">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The synthesized Co–NTA had been characterized by X-ray diffraction (XRD, Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information), of which the result agreed well with the reported articles.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202307645-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202307645-bib-0028_R_d124509684e588" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Mater. Today Chem.">28</a>, <a href="#adma202307645-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202307645-bib-0029_R_d124509684e588" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">29</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Scanning electron microscope (SEM) images in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202307645-fig-0001">1</a><span style="text-decoration:underline">b</span> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a> (Supporting Information) showed the thinner (55.6 ± 3.34&nbsp;nm) and more uniform Co–NTA nanowires as compared to the glass fiber separator (GF/D), and then when assembled to the separator, the Co-NWS has higher thinness (75 ± 2.68&nbsp;µm) and lighter weight (13.5&nbsp;mg per separator) than those of GF/D (675&nbsp;µm; 25.65&nbsp;mg per separator) (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information). These features could accelerate the Na ion migration via shortening transport distances, and constructing uniform and stable ion redistributors for homogenizing Na<sup>+</sup> deposition, effectively improving the Na ion conductivity and further reducing the Na dendrite nucleation and growth.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202307645-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202307645-bib-0030_R_d124509684e607" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">30</a>, <a href="#adma202307645-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202307645-bib-0031_R_d124509684e607" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Adv. Mater.">31</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Moreover, the Fourier transform infrared (FTIR) spectrum confirmed the abundant polar groups on the Co-NWS (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202307645-fig-0001">1c</a>), such as the ─COO─ (from the ─COOCo coordination group at 1682 and 1576 cm<sup>−1</sup>), C─O (1423 cm<sup>−1</sup>), and C─H (1121 cm<sup>−1</sup>), where those groups could provide better sodiophilic property and electrolyte wettability, favoring the Na<sup>+</sup> ion transport.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202307645-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202307645-bib-0032_R_d124509684e624" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Chemistry">32</a>-<a href="#adma202307645-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202307645-bib-0035_R_d124509684e624" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - RSC Adv.">35</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Furthermore, the nitrogen adsorption/desorption isotherms confirmed the larger surface area (24.12 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>−1</sup>) and more mesoporous structures in Co–NTA nanowires compared with the glass fiber counterpart (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>, Supporting Information), both of which could increase the contact area with electrolyte and thus improve the electrolyte wettability.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202307645-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202307645-bib-0036_R_d124509684e637" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">36</a>, <a href="#adma202307645-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202307645-bib-0037_R_d124509684e637" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - Energy Storage Mater.">37</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Benefiting from those advantages of the structure, Co-NWS could deliver a higher Na<sup>+</sup> ion transference number (<i>t</i><sub>Na+</sub>, 0.83) and ionic conductivity (1.393 mS cm<sup>−2</sup>) compared to the GF/D separators (<i>t</i><sub>Na+</sub> = 0.69; ionic conductivity of 1.209), demonstrating the stronger transport kinetics and thus suppressing the formation of Na dendrites during the Na plating/stripping process according to the space-charge theory (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202307645-fig-0001">1d</a> and Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a> (Supporting Information)).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202307645-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202307645-bib-0038_R_d124509684e663" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - Science">38</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Further, the Na ion diffusion coefficients (<i>D</i><sub>Na+</sub>) are quantitatively calculated based on the cyclic voltammogram (CV) methods under various scan rates (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a>, Supporting Information). The Na ion diffusion coefficient of Co-NWS was calculated to be 7.204 × 10<sup>−11</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup> at the reduction peak (R<sub>Co-NWS</sub> Peak) and 6.406 × 10<sup>−11</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup> at the oxidation peak (O<sub>Co-NWS</sub> Peak), both of which is over 2 times larger than the GF/D counterpart, furtherly revealing the special structure and chemical advantages of Co-NWS in Na ion transport kinetics.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202307645-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202307645-bib-0039_R_d124509684e692" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - Adv. Mater.">39</a>, <a href="#adma202307645-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202307645-bib-0040_R_d124509684e692" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - Adv. Energy Mater.">40</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Besides, the electrolyte wettability of separators has been evaluated by contact angle tests, as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202307645-fig-0001">1e</a>. The contact angle of Co-NWS was slightly larger than that of GF/D separators, which could be caused by the smaller macropore size and lower porosity of Co-NWS (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8</a> and Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202307645-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202307645-bib-0041_R_d124509684e707" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - J. Appl. Polym. Sci.">41</a>-<a href="#adma202307645-bib-0048" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202307645-bib-0048_R_d124509684e707" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 48 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">48</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> And due to the inherent hydrophobicity, the commercial PP/PE-based separators offered poor wettability for the polar carbonate electrolytes and thus could not serve as the separator in the SMBs with carbonate electrolytes (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9</a>, Supporting Information). Plus, the obtained Co-NWS displayed good mechanical flexibility, where no conspicuous damages could be observed after random folding or rolling (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202307645-fig-0001">1f</a>). Furthermore, mechanical properties, including tensile strength and Young's modules, have been measured, as shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10</a> (Supporting Information). Co-NWS showed higher mechanical performance, where its Young's modulus and tensile strength could reach 121.3 and 2.911&nbsp;MPa, respectively, demonstrating its stronger mechanically stiff nature compared with the GF/D separator. Besides, the X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) spectra of Co-NWS have also been collected, as indicated in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202307645-fig-0001">1g</a>, where those polar groups have demonstrated good consistency with the FTIR results.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202307645-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202307645-bib-0028_R_d124509684e725" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Mater. Today Chem.">28</a>, <a href="#adma202307645-bib-0049" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202307645-bib-0049_R_d124509684e725" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 49 - ACS Energy Lett.">49</a>-<a href="#adma202307645-bib-0055" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202307645-bib-0055_R_d124509684e725" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 55 - J. Phys. Chem. C">55</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Furthermore, the uniform distribution of the Co, N, C, and O elements on the surface of Co-NWS has also been proved by its energy-dispersive X-ray spectroscopy (EDS) mapping of the transmission electron microscopy image (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11</a>, Supporting Information). Furtherly, based on the results of EDS mapping and elemental analysis (Tables <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>, Supporting Information), the atomic ratio of the components, including N, C, H, O, and Co, has been obtained to be 1:6.25:7.3:6:1.37. Besides, the electrical conductivity of Co-NWS was also measured by an electrometer, as shown in Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a> (Supporting Information), confirming that the Co-NWS could afford enough electronic insulation properties and thus ensure the normal operation of the SMBs. Moreover, the thermal, chemical, and electrochemical stability of Co-NWS has also been checked. In detail, its thermal stability has been examined via the thermogravimetric analysis (TGA) curve (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12</a>, Supporting Information), where the slow mass loss of 3.6&nbsp;wt% below 200&nbsp;°C could be attributed to the evaporation of the absorbed water on the Co-NWS, and after the temperature over 312&nbsp;°C, the complete decomposition of Co-NWS has led to the well-defined weight loss of 77.4&nbsp;wt%.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202307645-bib-0056" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202307645-bib-0056_R_d124509684e746" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 56 - Dalton Trans.">56</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As for the&nbsp;chemical stability, Co-NWS was soaked in the carbonate electrolyte for 20 days, followed by vacuum drying at 80&nbsp;°C overnight, and then performed XRD to check its chemical information. Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13a</a> (Supporting Information) showed no obvious changes in the peak locations compared with the original results (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information), demonstrating its good chemical stability in the electrolyte. And the CV test from 0 to 5&nbsp;V was conducted on the Na||stainless steel (SS) cell with separators (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13b</a>, Supporting Information), where no extra oxidation or reduction peaks appeared on the Na|Co-NWS|SS cell in the voltage scope compared to the GF/D separator, demonstrating the good electrochemical stability of Co-NWS.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202307645-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202307645-bib-0040_R_d124509684e761" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - Adv. Energy Mater.">40</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Besides, the physical and chemical information of Co-NWS has been listed in Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a> (Supporting Information).</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adma202307645-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/79433a27-1c61-4733-acce-8dbbc2b92da4/adma202307645-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/79433a27-1c61-4733-acce-8dbbc2b92da4/adma202307645-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/b8be9b77-d5d7-4b23-ac02-d73863309d04/adma202307645-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/79433a27-1c61-4733-acce-8dbbc2b92da4/adma202307645-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202307645-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202307645" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Fabrication illustration and structural characterization of Co-NWS. a) Schematic of the synthesis procedure of Co-NWS. b) SEM image (scale bar: 500&nbsp;nm) and c) FTIR spectrum of Co-NWS (3425 cm<sup>−1</sup>: the coordination of Co<sup>+2</sup>; 2951 and 2917 cm<sup>−1</sup>: the stretching vibration of C─H; 1682 and 1576 cm<sup>−1</sup>: the ─COOCo coordination group; 1423 cm<sup>−1</sup>: C─O; 1247 cm<sup>−1</sup>: C─N). d) Chronoamperometry (CA) profiles of Na|Co-NWS|Na and Na|GF/D|Na cells with the carbonate electrolyte (1 <span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaPF<sub>6</sub> in PC/EC (v/v = 1:1) with 10&nbsp;wt% fluoroethylene carbonate (FEC)) and the corresponding <i>t</i><sub>Na+</sub> value. e) Contact angles of the carbonate electrolyte on Co-NWS and GF/D separators. f) Folding and rolling tests for Co-NWS. g) XPS spectra of Co-NWS.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>To investigate the Na plating and stripping behaviors in the carbonate electrolyte, the Na||Cu half-cells and the Na||Na symmetric cells with different separators have been assembled. As shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma202307645-fig-0002">2a</a>, the Co-NWS separator could enable SMBs with a stable CE of an average value of 97.9% for over 260 cycles, which could be attributed to the high reversibility of Na plating/stripping on cycling (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202307645-fig-0002">2b</a>). It should be noted that the average CE value could reach 98% after the 12th cycle (the orange part in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202307645-fig-0002">2a</a>), overwhelming most of the reported values of various strategies for stabilizing SMAs in the carbonate electrolytes (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information). On the contrary, the Na|GF/D|Cu displayed an unstable battery performance for about 50 cycles with 84.8% CE average value and poor reversibility of Na metal (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202307645-fig-0002">2c</a>) under the same conditions. Plus, due to the poor wettability to the carbonate electrolyte, the PP/PE-based separators displayed much worse cycling performance. Moreover, it should also be underlined that the amount of electrolyte the high-performance Na|Co-NWS|Cu cell needed was just 10&nbsp;µL, while 80&nbsp;µL for the GF/D counterpart, proving the high-efficiency utilization of electrolyte for Co-NWS. Further, the rate performance tests of Na||Cu cells with Co-NWS and GF/D separators at various current densities have also been performed, as shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14</a> (Supporting Information). Obviously, the asymmetric cell with GF/D showed about 80% CE value at the small current densities (0.5 and 1&nbsp;mA cm<sup>−2</sup>), and with the increase of current densities, its CE value continuously decreased, finally failed at 2&nbsp;mA cm<sup>−2</sup>, due to the unstable SEI interface and uncontrollable dendrite growth.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202307645-bib-0057" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202307645-bib-0057_R_d124509684e838" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 57 - Nano Res.">57</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In sharp contrast, CE of the Na||Cu cell with Co-NWS displayed an enlarging trend with the current densities increasing from 0.5 to 2&nbsp;mA cm<sup>−2</sup>, and then the CE value could continuous to go up and remain at 97% after the current densities turned back to 1.5 and 1&nbsp;mA cm<sup>−2</sup>, followed by slightly decrease to 96.3% at 0.5&nbsp;mA cm<sup>−2</sup>, confirming its high Na ion utilization efficiency during the Na cycling process at various current densities. Notably, the smaller amount of electrolyte, 5&nbsp;µL, has been investigated on the Na||Cu half-cell with the Co-NWS separator, as displayed in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S15</a> (Supporting Information), where the less electrolyte could also support the cell to reach over 120 stable cycles with an average CE value of with 96.1% at 0.5 and 0.5&nbsp;mA cm<sup>−2</sup>. Besides, the Na nucleation process has also been investigated based on the Na||Cu half-cell (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16</a>, Supporting Information), in which the cells with Co-NWS displayed a lower nucleation overpotential (118&nbsp;mV) than that of GF/D (192&nbsp;mV), verifying the improved Na-ion transport kinetics and the lower interface resistance on the Co-NWS.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202307645-bib-0058" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202307645-bib-0058_R_d124509684e859" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 58 - J. Energy Chem.">58</a>-<a href="#adma202307645-bib-0061" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202307645-bib-0061_R_d124509684e859" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 61 - Small">61</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In addition to the PC/EC/FEC electrolyte system, the Co-NWS separators could also be served in trimethyl-phosphate-based electrolyte systems and support the Na||Cu cell to reach higher CE value, demonstrating its broader applicability in the ester-based electrolytes (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S17</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adma202307645-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/9c21fc7e-2696-42a9-a8b0-f4553d0770be/adma202307645-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/9c21fc7e-2696-42a9-a8b0-f4553d0770be/adma202307645-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/eaed41ae-044e-4784-b91d-ea0b4dfc7b0b/adma202307645-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/9c21fc7e-2696-42a9-a8b0-f4553d0770be/adma202307645-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202307645-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202307645" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Electrochemical characterization of Na||Cu and Na||Na cells with different separators. a) CE of Na||Cu half-cells with different separators at 0.5&nbsp;mA cm<sup>−2</sup> and 0.5 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup>, and the corresponding voltage profiles of the cells with b) Co-NWS and c) GF/D separators. Time–voltage curves of the Na|Co-NWS|Na and Na|GF/D|Na symmetric cells at d) 0.5&nbsp;mA cm<sup>−2</sup> and 0.5 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup>, e) 1&nbsp;mA cm<sup>−2</sup> and 1 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup>, and f) 2&nbsp;mA cm<sup>−2</sup> and 1 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup>. g) The symmetric cells performance comparison between Na|Co-NWS|Na and other strategies reported in previous literature.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>To further assess and understand the advantages of the Co-NWS in protecting the SMAs, symmetric Na||Na cells have been detected. The cells with GF/D separators exhibited inferior cycle performance (less than 80 cycles) under 0.5 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup> with 0.5&nbsp;mA cm<sup>−2</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202307645-fig-0002">2d</a>), and apparent fluctuant voltage plateau when applying higher capacity and current density (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202307645-fig-0002">2e,f</a>). On the other hand, the Na|Co-NWS|Na showed stable cycle performance with uniform voltage plateaus over 500 cycles under 0.5 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup> with 0.5 mA cm<sup>−2</sup> and 1 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup> with 1 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup>, and could also maintain the reliable Na plating/stripping behavior even at a high current density of 2&nbsp;mA cm<sup>−2</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202307645-fig-0002">2f</a>), of which outstanding performance is also better than those of reported values (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202307645-fig-0002">2g</a>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202307645-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202307645-bib-0021_R_d124509684e935" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">21</a>, <a href="#adma202307645-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202307645-bib-0034_R_d124509684e935" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - J. Phys. Chem. C">34</a>, <a href="#adma202307645-bib-0062" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202307645-bib-0062_R_d124509684e935" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 62 - Small">62</a>-<a href="#adma202307645-bib-0071" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202307645-bib-0071_R_d124509684e935" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 71 - J. Mater. Chem. A">71</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Further, as shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S18</a> (Supporting Information), the rate performance of Na||Na symmetric cell with different separators at various current densities ranging from 0.5 to 3&nbsp;mA cm<sup>−2</sup> has been obtained. It should be noted that Co-NWS could effectively support the symmetric cell to reach a superior rate performance, compared to the GF/D counterpart. In particular, the symmetric cell with GF/D separator suddenly short-circuited at the current density of 2&nbsp;mA cm<sup>−2</sup> as the Na dendrites penetrated the separator, confirming the uneven sodium flux under a large current density and low mechanical resistance to avoid the separator puncture by dendrites. On the contrary, even stripping/plating at 3&nbsp;mA cm<sup>−2</sup>, the symmetric cell with Co-NWS could also deliver a stable cycling performance with only 82&nbsp;mV deposition voltage, and then the voltage also turned back to the pristine state after the current density became 0.5&nbsp;mA cm<sup>−2</sup>, illustrating the stable and uniform sodium flux at the rigorous conditions and the outstanding ion transfer properties of Co-NWS. To deeply understand those performances, the SEM images have checked the SMA status of two cells after 50 cycles (at 0.5 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup> with 0.5&nbsp;mA cm<sup>−2</sup>) and 100 cycles (at 1 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup> with 1&nbsp;mA cm<sup>−2</sup>). In detail, no Na dendrites and cracks have been observed on the SMA surface of the cell with Co-NWS after 50 cycles (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S19</a>, Supporting Information), and its surface could remain flat after 100 cycles at larger current densities (1&nbsp;mAh cm<sup>−2</sup> with&nbsp;1 mA cm<sup>−2</sup>, Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S20</a>, Supporting Information), while the GF/D counterpart showed a severely corroded morphology with many cracks, dendrites, and loose structures, and its surface morphology got worse with the increase of cycles and current densities, which could not only consume numerous amounts of electrolyte but also lead to sluggish Na<sup>+</sup> ion diffusion kinetics, finally displaying the inferior battery performance.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202307645-bib-0072" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202307645-bib-0072_R_d124509684e973" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 72 - Adv. Energy Mater.">72</a>-<a href="#adma202307645-bib-0075" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202307645-bib-0075_R_d124509684e973" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 75 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">75</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Besides, the electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) results before and after the cycling tests displayed the smaller charge-transfer resistance of the cell supported by Co-NWS as compared with the GF/D counterpart (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S21</a>, Supporting Information), further confirming the decreased Na<sup>+</sup> migration resistance and the fast charge-transfer kinetics of Co-NWS caused by the favorably sodiophilic and uniformly ion-redistributing network.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202307645-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202307645-bib-0031_R_d124509684e984" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Adv. Mater.">31</a>, <a href="#adma202307645-bib-0076" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202307645-bib-0076_R_d124509684e984" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 76 - Small Struct.">76</a>-<a href="#adma202307645-bib-0079" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202307645-bib-0079_R_d124509684e984" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 79 - Adv. Energy Mater.">79</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>To deeply investigate the effect of Co-NWS on SMBs and the mechanisms behind the better performance, finite element method (FEM) simulations and characterizations of cycled cells, including XPS, cross-section SEM, and atomic force microscopy (AFM), have been conducted, and the conclusion would be discussed from the following three aspects: First, as for morphology, compared with the GF/D separators with loose structure, uneven-size fibers, and caused large pore diameter, Co-NWS displayed denser packing of fibers with uniform size, leading to smaller pore size and lower porosity. Thus, the COMSOL simulations have been conducted on the Co-NWS and GF/D based on the abovementioned morphologies (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S22</a>, Supporting Information) to detect the influence of different morphologies on the Na plating/stripping process. Obviously, due to the relatively smaller and ordered nanopores, Co-NWS could deliver a homogenous electric field distribution, facilitating the formation of the uniform Na flux and thus avoiding the consumption of Na ions around the “hot spots,” finally leading to a reduction in the dendrites formation (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma202307645-fig-0003">3a–j</a>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202307645-bib-0080" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202307645-bib-0080_R_d124509684e1001" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 80 - Energy Storage Mater.">80</a>, <a href="#adma202307645-bib-0081" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202307645-bib-0081_R_d124509684e1001" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 81 - Adv. Energy Mater.">81</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, the potential distribution of GF/D separators was totally different from the Co-NWS counterpart, where the heterogeneous electric field distribution could be monitored and thus result in an uneven Na ion flux, favoring the formation and growth of dendrites.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202307645-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202307645-bib-0012_R_d124509684e1007" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Energy Storage Mater.">12</a>, <a href="#adma202307645-bib-0080" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202307645-bib-0080_R_d124509684e1007" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 80 - Energy Storage Mater.">80</a>, <a href="#adma202307645-bib-0082" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202307645-bib-0082_R_d124509684e1007" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 82 - Adv. Energy Mater.">82</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> With the increasing deposition time, the flat Na plating could be achieved under the assistance of Co-NWS, while GF/D induced the uneven Na deposition, where the opposite phenomenon was directly caused by the abovementioned difference in electric field distribution and Na flux. And to further confirm the simulation results, the Na||Cu half-cells assembled by Co-NWS and GF/D separators have been conducted the Na plating procedure with different areal capacities, and then the cross-section SEM images of the Na deposited Cu foil have been taken, as displayed in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202307645-fig-0003">3k–p</a>. Just after 0.25 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup> plating, the Na dendrites could be observed on the Cu foil under the GF/D separator, and those dendrites could continuously grow with increasing areal capacity. Remarkably, the deposited Na layer remains flat under the Co-NWS separator even after 1 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup> plating. Those results agreed well with the simulation, which confirmed the effective and uniformly ion-distributing role of Co-NWS, fundamentally reducing the formation of Na dendrites and thus improving the CE value. As for further investigating the ability of Co-NWS to redistribute Na ions uniformly during the Na cycling process, the Na||Cu cells with different separators have been performed 30 cycles first and then conducted the Na deposition process at the current densities of 0.5&nbsp;mA cm<sup>−2</sup> for 4&nbsp;h (2 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup>), and the corresponding cross-section SEM images of Cu sides have been shooting, as displayed in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S23</a> (Supporting Information). Obviously, after cycling and plating procedure, the Cu side under Co-NWS owned more flat and uniform Na deposition morphology; however, many Na dendrites and dead Na have been found in the Cu side with the GF/D separator, of which results agreed well with the Na||Cu cell performance (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202307645-fig-0002">2</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14</a> (Supporting Information)) and the FEM simulations results (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202307645-fig-0003">3a–j</a>). Moreover, the maximum capacity test for Na||Cu half-cells showed the larger maximum capacity of the cell with Co-NWS, compared with the GF/D counterpart (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S24</a>, Supporting Information), further confirming its strong ability to uniform distribution of Na ions. And it was also proved that the nanoporous structure of Co-NWS could not be filled with Na metal during the cycling process.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adma202307645-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/e17c5ff5-3323-4197-873c-5fc3b43664ed/adma202307645-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/e17c5ff5-3323-4197-873c-5fc3b43664ed/adma202307645-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/bb56ff12-4223-4df2-a598-c0d830aae93f/adma202307645-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/e17c5ff5-3323-4197-873c-5fc3b43664ed/adma202307645-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202307645-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202307645" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Simulation diagram and characterization of half-cells after cycles. FEM simulations on the potential distributions of a) GF/D and f) Co-NWS (scale bars: potential (V)). The plating procedure with different separators after a specific time, i.e., b,g) 0 s, c,h) 100 s, d,i) 300 s, and e,j) 600 s (scale bars: Na<sup>+</sup> concentration (mol m<sup>−3</sup>)); k–p) the cross-section SEM images of Cu foil in Na||Cu cells with the different separators (scale bar: 5&nbsp;µm) after (k,n) 0.25 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup>, (i,o) 0.5 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup>, (m,p) 1 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup> Na plating.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>Besides, regarding the chemical properties, as displayed by the FTIR spectrum of Co-NWS (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202307645-fig-0001">1c</a>), its surface has abundant functional groups. To investigate the effects of those groups on the SEI construction, the depth profiling XPS technique has been performed on the Na anode after 50 cycles, as displayed in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma202307645-fig-0004">4a</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S25</a> (Supporting Information). As for the SEI layer of SMAs in Na|Co-NWS|Na cell, the peaks located at 284.8&nbsp;eV (C─C), 287.8&nbsp;eV (─CO<sub>2</sub>─), and 289.4&nbsp;eV (CO<sub>3</sub><sup>2−</sup>) in the spectrum of C 1s, as well as the peaks at 530.7&nbsp;eV (Na─O), 531.6&nbsp;eV (C═O), and 533&nbsp;eV (C─O) in the spectrum of O 1s could be pointed out the components decomposed from the carbonate electrolytes, which was in line with the GF/D counterparts (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S25</a>, Supporting Information).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202307645-bib-0067" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202307645-bib-0067_R_d124509684e1093" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 67 - Nano Lett.">67</a>, <a href="#adma202307645-bib-0080" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202307645-bib-0080_R_d124509684e1093" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 80 - Energy Storage Mater.">80</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Regarding the F 1s spectra, the peaks at 684.3 and 687.3&nbsp;eV could be pointed as the Na─F and P─F bonds, respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202307645-bib-0073" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202307645-bib-0073_R_d124509684e1098" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 73 - Adv. Energy Mater.">73</a>, <a href="#adma202307645-bib-0080" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202307645-bib-0080_R_d124509684e1098" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 80 - Energy Storage Mater.">80</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Interestingly, the SEI (0 s) with the Co-NWS separator displayed a weaker P─F peak (8.3% of F content) compared with the GF/D counterpart (44.1% of F content), which could be attributed to the unstable PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> structures broken derived from the polarization of the P─F bond induced by Co-NWS, demonstrating the critical role of those polar groups on inducing a NaF-rich SEI during the Na plating/stripping process.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202307645-bib-0073" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202307645-bib-0073_R_d124509684e1107" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 73 - Adv. Energy Mater.">73</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Furthermore, as shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S26</a> (Supporting Information), the relationship between the C, O, F contents in the SEI layer and the sputtering time has been described. In detail, as for the formed SEI layers with Co-NWS, the atomic ratio of organic species (ROCO<sub>2</sub>Na) reduced whereas that of inorganic species, such as Na<sub>2</sub>O and NaF, increased gradually, along with the sputtering time increasing from 0 to 200 s. Notably, with the increasing sputtering time, the content of F in the SEI layer of Co-NWS was also raised gradually, and the P─F bonds have almost completely disappeared, which means the continuously increased Na─F content (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S26</a>, Supporting Information). Whereas in the SEI layer with GF/D, although its F content also increased with the development of etching time, it was still lower than the Co-NWS counterpart. Besides, the obvious P─F could still be detected after 200 s etching, demonstrating the less Na─F content and the weak ability of GF/D on the polarization of P─F bonds. The NaF-rich SEI layers have been confirmed to improve the Na<sup>+</sup> transport and effectively restrict the dendrite growth of Na, which could enable SMBs to achieve better cycling performance.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202307645-bib-0082" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202307645-bib-0082_R_d124509684e1125" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 82 - Adv. Energy Mater.">82</a>-<a href="#adma202307645-bib-0084" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202307645-bib-0084_R_d124509684e1125" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 84 - Energy Storage Mater.">84</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Besides, a small amount of N elements has been detected in the SEI layers of the Na|Co-NWS|Na cell, and those peaks at 398, 398.7, 399.8, and 400.7&nbsp;eV could be attributed to the formed Na<sub>3</sub>N (Na─N bonds), pyridinc N, N<i><sub>x</sub></i>O<i><sub>y</sub></i><sup>−</sup>, and pyrrolic N species in the SEI layer, respectively, where peaks still existed after 200 s depth profiling, proving successful construction of the N-containing SEI layer.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202307645-bib-0085" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202307645-bib-0085_R_d124509684e1140" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 85 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">85</a>-<a href="#adma202307645-bib-0090" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202307645-bib-0090_R_d124509684e1140" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 90 - Chem. Rev.">90</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As for the Na<sub>3</sub>N and NaN<i><sub>x</sub></i>O<i><sub>y</sub></i>, both could serve as good Na<sup>+</sup> conductors and effectively reduce the nonuniform formation of Na dendrite, supporting better cycling performance.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202307645-bib-0089" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202307645-bib-0089_R_d124509684e1156" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 89 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">89</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> It should be mentioned that the N signals did not originate from the Co–NTA nanowires remaining on the SEI film, as there are no Co signals in the XPS spectra (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S27</a>, Supporting Information). On the contrary, when applying the GF/D separator to the symmetric cell, no N signals existed in the corresponding SEI layers (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S28</a>, Supporting Information), further confirming that Co-NWS could help the formation of the N-containing SEI layers. Therefore, benefitting from those functional groups, Co-NWS could facilitate the construction of N,F-rich SEI layer in the SMBs, enabling the SEI layer with higher elastic modulus to reduce the risk of cracks and faster Na ion transport kinetics, and thus reaching high CE value and long battery life span.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202307645-bib-0090" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202307645-bib-0090_R_d124509684e1168" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 90 - Chem. Rev.">90</a>-<a href="#adma202307645-bib-0093" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202307645-bib-0093_R_d124509684e1168" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 93 - ACS Energy Lett.">93</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Finally, the good mechanical properties of separators could effectively protect the dendrites from penetrating the separator and thus avoid short circuits during the long-term Na plating/stripping cycles. Herein, the rigid silicon probe of AFM was applied to put pressure on Co-NWS to simulate the puncture of the dendrites into the separator. The force applied to the probe was increased gradually, and the corresponding force–displacement curves have been recorded, as depicted in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202307645-fig-0004">4b</a>. The plastic deformation was recognized to occur when the retracting curve and the extending curve cannot overlap with each other. Obviously, Co-NWS retained the elastic deformation with Young's modulus of 6.12 ± 0.408&nbsp;GPa when the applied force of the probe was 166 nN, and this average Young's modulus was higher than the pristine Na (about 3&nbsp;GPa), displaying its robust puncture resistance capability.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202307645-bib-0094" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202307645-bib-0094_R_d124509684e1177" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 94 - Adv. Mater.">94</a>-<a href="#adma202307645-bib-0096" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202307645-bib-0096_R_d124509684e1177" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 96 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">96</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Based on those results, the reasons for the excellent battery performance of cells with Co-NWS could be attributed to the following aspects: 1) Co-NWS could serve as a good distributor for the carbonate electrolyte, regulating the uniform flux of Na<sup>+</sup> ion and thus reducing the possibility of Na dendrite nucleation; 2) the functional groups of Co-NWS could furtherly help construct a strong N,F-rich SEI during the Na stripping/plating process, enhancing the Na<sup>+</sup> transport and limiting the Na dendrite growth; 3) the solid mechanical strength of Co-NWS could effectively protect itself from piercing by the Na dendrites, avoiding the short-circuit phenomenon (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202307645-fig-0004">4c</a>).</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adma202307645-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/bc5cd794-bee9-48f0-adab-78bcf4467238/adma202307645-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/bc5cd794-bee9-48f0-adab-78bcf4467238/adma202307645-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/4e873d47-247e-4edc-af1b-28d95c758e33/adma202307645-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/bc5cd794-bee9-48f0-adab-78bcf4467238/adma202307645-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202307645-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202307645" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Characterization of SEIs after cycles. a) XPS spectra of SEI in the Na|Co-NWS|Na symmetric cell after 50 cycles. b) Typical force–displacement curves of Co-NWS. c) Schematic illustration of Na plating process for the cell with Co-NWS and GF/D.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>Further, the full cells, Na||NVP, have been assembled with the Co-NWS and GF/D separators to evaluate their applicability in practical SMBs. First, the rate performances of Na|Co-NWS|NVP and Na|GF/D|NVP full cells from 0.5 C to 5 C have been checked, as depicted in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma202307645-fig-0005">5a</a>. As the increase of current density, the full cell with the GF/D separator exhibited a much lower specific capacity density than the cell using Co-NWS, and when the current density finally returned to 0.5 C, the Na|Co-NWS|NVP cell still displayed stable cycling with a higher specific capacity. The enhanced rate performance could be attributed to the rapid Na<sup>+</sup> ion transport of Co-NWS.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202307645-bib-0073" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202307645-bib-0073_R_d124509684e1219" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 73 - Adv. Energy Mater.">73</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Besides, the corresponding voltage profiles have been shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202307645-fig-0005">5b</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S29</a> (Supporting Information), where the Na|Co-NWS|NVP cell showed lower voltage polarization than the GF/D counterpart, benefiting from its outstanding interface stability.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202307645-bib-0080" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202307645-bib-0080_R_d124509684e1231" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 80 - Energy Storage Mater.">80</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Furthermore, the life span at different current densities for the two full cells has also been investigated (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202307645-fig-0005">5c,d</a>). In detail, the Na|Co-NWS|NVP cell performed stable cycling over 5000 cycles at 1 C with an average CE value of 99.9% and a capacity retention of 50.84%, whereas the Na|GF/D|NVP cell displayed very short cycling performance, about 500 cycles, at the same current density. And after increasing the current density to 5 C, the full cell with Co-NWS could also maintain excellent stability over 1500 cycles with the CE of 99.8% and the capacity retention of 92%, which far-away surpassed the performance of the GF/D counterpart (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202307645-fig-0005">5d</a>). Furthermore, the full cells with stricter conditions, i.e., with a relatively high loading of cathodes (about 5&nbsp;mg cm<sup>−2</sup>) and limited Na anodes based on an N/P ratio of 7:1, have been recorded, as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202307645-fig-0005">5e</a>. The Na|Co-NWS|NVP cell could maintain stable performance for more than 170 cycles with a high CE value (98.7%) and high capacity retention (88.3%). By contrast, the full cell with the GF separator showed an obvious decay after 47 cycles and then short-circuited at 61 cycles due to the punctured separator by Na dendrites. As for further simulating actual working conditions, the full cells with Co-NWS and GF/D separators have been examined with lower N/P ratio (3:1) at 1 C, as shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S30</a> (Supporting Information), where the full cell with Co–NTA could maintain a stable cycling performance over 70 cycles with high CE value of 98.7% and 92.7% capacity retention, while the GF/D counterpart affords less than 40 cycles with 97.1% CE and 37.6% capacity retention. Those excellent performances of the Na|Co-NWS|NVP cell have surpassed most of the reported SMBs with carbonate electrolytes (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a>, Supporting Information), and further confirmed the effective protection on the SMAs from the Co-NWS separators.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202307645-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202307645-bib-0014_R_d124509684e1254" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Adv. Mater.">14</a>, <a href="#adma202307645-bib-0097" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202307645-bib-0097_R_d124509684e1254" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 97 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">97</a>-<a href="#adma202307645-bib-0099" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202307645-bib-0099_R_d124509684e1254" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 99 - J. Energy Chem.">99</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adma202307645-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/5c110560-ead6-42e6-95fc-b71b97527b22/adma202307645-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/5c110560-ead6-42e6-95fc-b71b97527b22/adma202307645-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/b7f98dc9-af4e-4757-9ed9-14a9d5f8ca1c/adma202307645-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/5c110560-ead6-42e6-95fc-b71b97527b22/adma202307645-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202307645-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202307645" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Electrochemical performance of Na||NVP full cells with different separators. a) Rate performance comparison of full cells with Co-NWS and GF/D separators, and b) the corresponding charging/discharging profiles. Cycling performance of full cells with different separators at c) 1 C and d) 5 C. e) Cycle performance of NVP||Na cells with Co-NWS and GF/D separators with a N/P ratio of 7:1 at 1 C.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
      </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adma202307645-sec-0030">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adma202307645-sec-0030-title">3 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In conclusion, a functionalized separator, Co-NWS, with lighter weight and higher thinness, has been successfully developed for SMBs via a simple and time-saving preparation. The separator realized to full-chain alleviates the formation and growth of Na dendrites, i.e., uniformly directing Na ion flux to reduce the dendrite formation; helping construct the strong SEI layer to reduce the risk of cracks and enhance the Na ions transport kinetics; directly preventing dendrites from penetrating the separator through the properties of high elastic modulus. Thanks to those features, the developed separator could enable a long life span with a high average CE of 98% for the Na||Cu half-cell and over 500&nbsp;h stable cycling for the Na||Na symmetric cells under 0.5 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup>. Remarkably, the outstanding performance of the cells only required 10&nbsp;µL carbonated electrolyte. Furthermore, the long-term cycling of over 1500 cycles with a high capacity retention of 92% could be achieved at the high rate (5 C) for the full cells with Co-NWS, and even when the N/P ratio shrank to 7:1, the cell could also deliver 172 stable cycles with high CE and capacity values. The advantages of Co-NWS not only could support SMBs to achieve a dendrite-free SMA under the carbonate electrolyte, but also provide an effective strategy for increasing the mass and volumetric energy densities via saving the weight and volume of separators and the electrolyte usage. This work offers new guidance and sights for realizing low-cost, high-performance, and high-energy-density rechargeable SMBs in the future.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adma202307645-sec-0040">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adma202307645-sec-0040-title">4 Experimental Section</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202307645-sec-0050">
            
            <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202307645-sec-0060">
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202307645-sec-0070">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adma202307645-sec-0070-title"> Synthesis of the Co-NWS Separator</h4>
                  
                  <p>In a typical synthesis, CoCl<sub>2</sub> (0.77&nbsp;g) was dissolved first into deionized water (30&nbsp;mL), followed by stirring for 10 min, and then NTA (0.6&nbsp;g) and 2-propanol (10&nbsp;mL) were added into the solution. After stirring for 15 min, the solution was transferred into a Teflon-lined stainless-steel autoclave to react at 180&nbsp;°C for 6&nbsp;h. Subsequently, the obtained Co–NTA nanofiber was washed with deionized water 3 times. Finally, the Co-NWS membrane was obtained via vacuum filtering, followed by vacuum drying at 60&nbsp;°C overnight.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202307645-sec-0080">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adma202307645-sec-0080-title"> Material Characterization</h4>
                  
                  <div class="paragraph-element">The XRD measurements were performed on PANalytical Aeris X-ray diffractometer with Cu Kα radiation (<i>λ</i> = 1.5406 Å) at a scanning rate of 2° min<sup>−1</sup>. The morphology and structure of samples were checked by the field-emission scanning electron microscope (JEOL JSM-7500FA) and scanning transmission electron microscope (JEOL JEM-ARM200F). XPS characterization was conducted on a Thermo Scientific Nexsa X-ray photoelectron spectrometer, and all peaks were calibrated by the C 1s peak at 284.8&nbsp;eV. AFM data were collected via the atomic force microscope (Bruker Bioscope Resolve). FTIR results were obtained on the PerkinElmer Frontiers instrument. TGA was conducted on TA Instruments 2000 under an Ar atmosphere from 50 to 600&nbsp;°C with a heating rate of 5&nbsp;°C min<sup>−</sup><sup>1</sup>. The surface area and pore size analysis were characterized by the Micromeritics Tristar II 3020 surface area and porosity analyzer. The contact angle tests were conducted on the DataPhysics OCA 25 instrument with the carbonate electrolyte (1.0&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaPF<sub>6</sub> in PC:EC = 1:1 v/v, with 10&nbsp;wt% FEC). EIS tests were recorded using the Biologic VMP3 electrochemical workstation. The ion conductivity was characterized by the symmetric SS cells, i.e., SS|separator|SS. EIS was conducted from 0.01&nbsp;Hz to 100&nbsp;kHz with a constant amplitude of 10&nbsp;mV. The ionic conductivity (<i>σ</i>) could be calculated via the following equation

The electrical conductivity of Co-NWS was measured by an electrometer (6517A, Keithley, USA). Tensile properties were quantitated by a tensile stage controller (Shimadzu EZ mechanical tester, Japan) with a 10 N load cell at a crosshead speed of 1&nbsp;mm min<sup>−1</sup>. The macroporous size was measured by mercury intrusion porosimetry (MIP, Micromeritics Autopore V 9620).                   <div class="paragraph-element">The Na ion diffusion coefficient was calculated based on the Randles–Sevick equation

                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202307645-sec-0090">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adma202307645-sec-0090-title"> Electrochemical Testing</h4>
                  
                  <p>All coin-type cells (2032) were assembled in the Ar-filled glove box (O<sub>2</sub> &lt; 0.1&nbsp;ppm; H<sub>2</sub>O &lt; 0.1&nbsp;ppm). The electrolyte contained 1.0&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaPF<sub>6</sub> in PC:EC = 1:1 v/v, with 10&nbsp;wt% FEC. As for cells with Co-NWS, the liquid electrolyte used in a half-cell/symmetric cell was 10&nbsp;µL, and a full cell was 20&nbsp;µL, while the cells with the GF/D separators were 80&nbsp;µL. The Na||Cu half-cells were assembled to check the CE with a cut-off voltage of 0.3&nbsp;V, and the symmetric cells were assembled to evaluate the SMA reversibility with different separators. NVP was synthesized according to previous literature.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202307645-bib-0101" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202307645-bib-0101_R_d124509684e1581" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 101 - Adv. Energy Mater.">101</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> NVP electrodes were prepared by blade coating the slurry of NVP, super P, and PVDF with a mass ratio of 8:1:1 on the Al foil, followed by vacuum drying overnight. The Na anode for the full cell with a limited N/P ratio was prepared by pre-electrodeposition in the Na||Cu half-cell with the carbonate electrolyte.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202307645-sec-0100">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adma202307645-sec-0100-title"> COMSOL Simulations</h4>
                  
                  <p>The potential distributions and Na deposition procedure were simulated by COMSOL Multiphysics 5.4 with the implemented finite element method. The models were constructed based on the SEM images, assuming the relatively ordered geometry with high-density nanowires for Co-NWS, and greatly disordered geometry with low-density fibers for GF/D. The simulation was conducted by Tertiary Current Distribution, Nernst–Planck module in COMSOL, assuming that the Na deposition occurred with 100% current yield following the reaction: Na<sup>+</sup> + e<sup>−</sup> → Na, and there were no other side reactions. The deposited potential was 0.5&nbsp;V, and the Na<sup>+</sup> concentration was 1&nbsp;mol m<sup>−3</sup>. A time-dependent simulation model was used to account for the movement of the cathode boundary during the deposition process. To simplify the analysis, a 2D model was applied.</p>
                  </section>
               </section>
            </section>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">
